Schoenfeldt v. Municipal Court of San Francisco


Civ. No. 19238


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


187 Cal. App. 2d 748; 9 Cal. Rptr. 912; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1453



December 28, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Orla St. Clair, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed on motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought a writ of prohibition seeking to prohibit appellee municipal court from taking any further steps to enforce a traffic offense conviction against her. The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) denied the writ and appellant filed an appeal. The municipal court moved for dismissal of the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant contended based her request for a writ of prohibition upon her argument that the statute under which she was convicted was unconstitutional. The grounds of the motion to dismiss were that petitioner had a plain, speedy and adequate remedy by appeal, and that because judgment had been pronounced and no appeal taken therefrom, there remained no further judicial function to be performed by the municipal court. The municipal court contended that the issuance of a writ of prohibition not serve any useful purpose. On appeal, the court agreed, holding that appellant had pursued the wrong remedy. The court reasoned that all of the matters that appellant attempted to raise on her application for writ of prohibition could have been and should have been raised upon an appeal from the judgment of the municipal court. The court concluded that the writ of prohibition could not be used to take the place of an appeal or other mode of review even though objections thereto were based upon jurisdictional grounds.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed.

 

1557 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


County of Alameda v. Superior Court of Alameda County


Civ. No. 19622


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


187 Cal. App. 2d 502; 10 Cal. Rptr. 84; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1418



December 19, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Alameda County and Cecil Mosbacher, Judge thereof, to dismiss an appeal from a small claims court judgment.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county commenced a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondents, a superior court and a superior court judge, to dismiss an appeal from a small claims judgment which was entered in the county's favor in the county's action against the real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: The real party in interest filed an appeal of the judgment in favor of the county in the superior court on May 18, 1959. Because the action had not been brought to trial, the county filed a motion in the superior court to dismiss the appeal. The superior court denied the motion. The county then filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel the superior court to dismiss the appeal. The court held that Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 117jj (1959) was added to provided that appeals had to be dismissed where the appealing party failed to bring the appeal to trial within one year from the date of filing the appeal in the superior court. Thus, dismissal was voluntary after the expiration of one year. The burden was on the appealing party to show that he had been unable, from causes beyond his control, to bring the case to trial within one year. The legislation limiting the time for performance of a procedural act was not unconstitutional if adequate means of enforcing the remedy remained. Although § 117jj was added four months after the appeal was filed, the remaining eight months for the real party in interest to bring the appeal was amply if due diligence were exercised.


OUTCOME: The court held that granting of the county's motion to dismiss was mandatory and it issued the peremptory writ of mandate directing dismissal of the appeal.

 

1558 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Benedetti v. Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control


Civ. No. 19205


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


187 Cal. App. 2d 213; 9 Cal. Rptr. 525; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1374



December 8, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 6, 1961.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Orla St. Clair, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to review a decision of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control revoking a liquor license.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant license holder challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) vacating a writ and denying a peremptory writ of mandate, thereby approving respondent California Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board's ruling upholding an order of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control (DABC) that adopted a hearing officer's findings that the holder violated the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act.


OVERVIEW: Finding that the holder had violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25601 and § 24200, subd. (e), a DABC hearing officer revoked his license. The officer also ruled that the holder's retention of the license would be contrary to public welfare and morals within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22. Though the Board reversed a part of the ruling, it sustained revocation based on the § 25601 violation. The superior court ultimately upheld the Board's decision and denied the writ. The holder appealed, claiming that there was no proof of knowledge on the part of the holder or its agents of the commission of proscribed acts, and that the evidence in any case was insufficient to prove knowledge. The court affirmed. It held that under § 25601, "permit" did not mean knowledge but it in fact implied no affirmative act, involved no intent, and was satisfied by mere passivity on the holder's part. Moreover, "knowledge" was proved for statutory purposes by a mere showing that the proscribed acts occurred. Finally, the court noted that the revocation was properly affirmed in any event on the basis of the alternate constitutional ground. Accordingly, the revocation was upheld.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court denying a peremptory writ of mandate to the license holder.

 

1559 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Jacob v. Hibernia Bank


Civ. Nos. 18695, 18777, 18778, 18779


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


186 Cal. App. 2d 756; 9 Cal. Rptr. 901; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1690



November 28, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 27, 1960, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 27, 1961. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Milton D. Sapiro, Judge.

Actions seeking a declaration of rights in a bank.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Summary judgments for defendants, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, plaintiffs in four separate lawsuits, complained of the judgments of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted defendant bank's motion for summary judgment in plaintiffs' claims for a judgment declaring them to be owners of the bank.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs each brought a declaratory judgment action against the bank, seeking to be declared members, or owners, of the bank. The claim of right of each plaintiff was by inheritance through deceased relatives, who were at one time members of the bank. The trial court granted the motion for summary judgment for the bank in each of the four cases. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that no depositor of a bank has rights that were remotely similar to that of an owner. The court held that plaintiffs' claims through inheritance failed because, in each case, the account that originally formed the basis for the membership, or ownership of the bank, had been closed. When plaintiffs later opened their own accounts, the bank had changed its bylaws to eliminate the benefit that, by opening an account, the account holder became a share holder, or member, of the bank.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted the motion for summary judgment of the bank in the plaintiffs' actions seeking to be declared owners of the bank.

 

1560 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lawton


Crim. No. 7235


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


186 Cal. App. 2d 834; 9 Cal. Rptr. 122; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1702



November 28, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 25, 1961.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. William E. Fox, Judge.

Prosecution for unlawfully possessing heroin.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him in a nonjury trial of unlawfully possessing heroin, denied his motion for a new trial, and sentenced him to state prison.


OVERVIEW: Two informers told officers that a person known by a specified nickname was selling heroin on a specified street. Other police officers told them that the person known by the nickname was defendant. They found that defendant had a prior narcotics record. The officer observed defendant talking to known narcotics users who told them that defendant was dealing in narcotics up an down the same street as was indicated by the informers. When the officers learned defendant's address, they went to that address in plain clothes. As defendant opened the door to his apartment, they approached him. He ran in and closed the door. After they forced the door open, they saw defendant coming from the bathroom. They conducted a search and found balloons of heroin. Defendant told them that it was for its own use. The court held, on appeal, that there was probable cause for the arrest because there was enough to arouse in the minds of the officers a suspicion founded on circumstances that would warrant a reasonable man in the belief that defendant was in the possession of narcotics. Because there was probable cause for the arrest, it was legal and the search incident thereto was also legal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendant of unlawfully possessing heroin.

Dittus v. Cranston


Civ. No. 10038


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


186 Cal. App. 2d 837; 9 Cal. Rptr. 314; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1703



November 28, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 23, 1960, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 25, 1961.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the State Controller to draw a warrant in favor of the Secretary of the State Board of Control for a specified amount.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner secretary of the state board of control filed a writ of mandamus to compel respondent state controller to pay its claim for a specific amount.


OVERVIEW: The secretary sought to recover funds that were appropriated to her office by the Budget Act of 1959. The controller honored her request for all but two of the items. The controller alleged that those two items constituted an unconstitutional use of public funds. One item involved a request by a senator's widow for the money that her husband would have earned had he served his entire term of office. The secretary filed a mandamus to compel the controller to pay the remaining claim. The court found that there was nothing on the face of the Act that supported the controller's reason for denying the claim. The court found that the Act was valid, constitutional, and required the controller to pay the secretary's claim.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ.

 

1562 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. San Bernardino


Civ. No. 6332


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District


186 Cal. App. 2d 603; 9 Cal. Rptr. 431; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1673



November 22, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 18, 1961.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Jesse W. Curtis, Jr., Judge.

Action for declaratory and other relief.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that held for defendants, city and municipal water district, and denied relief to the taxpayer in his action for a judgment declaring that the city was not part of the district and restraining the district from levying or assessing taxes or liens on property situated in the city.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that the city was included in the district as a result of an election, that the taxpayer's proceeding was time-barred by § 11 of the Municipal Water District Act of 1911 (Act), that the required proceeding was one in quo warranto and that the attorney general had not consented to such an action, and that the district's formation was not free from interference by laws of the legislature as a municipal affair within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XI, §§ 6, 8. The court affirmed the judgment. The organization of a municipal water district by a larger area that included a city was not a municipal affair and came within the domain and regulation of the state's general laws. Quo warranto was the correct proceeding in which to attack the regularity of the organization of a municipal or public corporation such as a water district, and an individual taxpayer could not bring such an action. Section 11 of the Act was a statute of limitations, not a curative act, and it applied to bar the taxpayer's action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that denied relief to the taxpayer in his action for a judgment declaring that the city was not part of the district and restraining the district from levying or assessing taxes or liens on property in the city.

 

1563 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Arnke v. Berkeley


Civ. No. 19283


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


185 Cal. App. 2d 842; 8 Cal. Rptr. 645; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1587



November 1, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 1, 1960, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied December 28, 1960. Schauer, J., McComb, J., and White, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Marvin Sherwin, Judge.

Proceeding for declaratory relief to have a city ordinance declared unconstitutional.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant businessman challenged an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that found Berkeley, Cal., Ordinance 2805-N.S. entitled Licensing for Purposes of Revenue Certain Professions, Businesses, Trades and Occupations in the City of Berkeley valid and constitutional in all respects.


OVERVIEW: The businessman was a contractor licensed to do business in California. Some of his business was done within respondent city, and under Berkeley, Cal., Ordinance 2805-N.S., he was required to pay a license fee. The business filed an action for declaratory relief to have the city ordinance declared unconstitutional, but the trial court determined that it was valid and enforceable in all respects. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the businessman's claim that the state had occupied the field of regulation was inapplicable because the city sought to enforce its licensing ordinance for revenue purposes only, not regulation. The fact that the city required the businessman to affix a sticker to each of his vehicles as an aid to enforcement did not make the tax a regulatory one. The court refused to rule on the validity of the section that required a license before doing a single act of business because the businessman, doing one-sixth of his business in the city, was not a party aggrieved by any possible application of that section. Even if that provision was illegal, it did not affect the validity of the remaining portions under Berkeley, Cal., Ordinance 2805-N.S. § 8.1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order declaring a city licensing ordinance valid and constitutional.

 

1564 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kelly v. Mahoney


Civ. No. 9904


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


185 Cal. App. 2d 799; 8 Cal. Rptr. 521; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1582



October 31, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Merced County. R. R. Sischo, Judge.

Proceeding in prohibition to restrain the Justice Court of Livingston Judicial District and J. H. Mahoney, Judge thereof, from further proceeding in a criminal action.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment granting writ, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a judge and the Justice Court of Livingston Judicial District, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Merced County (California) that granted a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining appellants from further proceeding in an action charging respondent, a turkey rancher, with a violation of Merced Co., California Ordinance No. 309 § 3.01(H).


OVERVIEW: The rancher was charged with violating the section of the ordinance requiring cover crops or other dust control methods on turkey farms. The trial court held that appellants had no jurisdiction to try the rancher for the alleged offense because the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court held that the ordinance was a valid exercise of the police power and did not deprive the rancher of his property without due process of law. The ordinance was sufficiently explicit to inform turkey ranchers what was required of them, and it was unreasonable to suppose that any turkey rancher in an agricultural zone who was possessed of ordinary intelligence could misunderstand its meaning. The finding of the trial court that the part of the ordinance under attack was unreasonable and discriminatory in that it did not apply to the raising of chickens on chicken ranches could not be sustained. The question of whether or not the chicken ranches created a sufficient problem to require the same restrictions as were applied to turkey ranches was a question of policy for the board of supervisors to decide, and the trial court was not justified in holding that the classification was unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1565 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Shaffer v. Justice Court for Chico Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 9808


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


185 Cal. App. 2d 405; 8 Cal. Rptr. 269; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1517



October 17, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 4, 1960, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied December 14, 1960.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County. A. B. Ware, Judge.

Proceeding in prohibition to restrain a justice court from further proceedings in a criminal case.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment granting writ, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant justice court challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which granted respondent a peremptory writ of prohibition commanding the justice court to desist and refrain absolutely from any further proceeding in a criminal case against respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with wilfully and unlawfully acting in the capacity of a contractor, without having been issued a license by the registrar of Construction of the State of California. In his petition for the writ of prohibition, respondent alleged that the statute under which he was charged, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7028, was unconstitutional and void and that it was thus ineffective to confer jurisdiction upon the justice court. In reversing the trial court's judgment, the court ruled that a tribunal had jurisdiction to determine its own jurisdiction. Respondent had not in the justice court challenged the jurisdiction of that court by demurrer, motion, plea, or other objection so that the justice court could have preliminarily decided the question of whether it had jurisdiction. Therefore, there was nothing for the trial court to prohibit. Respondent's allegation that the justice court had denied respondent's plea of unconstitutionality in an earlier case did not validate the writ. A party could not assert the outcome of a ruling or decision that a trial court had not even been permitted to make.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment.

Hesperia Land Development Co. v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


Civ. No. 24809


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


184 Cal. App. 2d 865; 7 Cal. Rptr. 815; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1945



September 27, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 25, 1960, and Petitioners' Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied November 23, 1960.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and Joseph W. Vickers, Judge thereof, to vacate an order of dismissal and reinstate a temporary restraining order.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner developers sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondents, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) and a judge, to vacate an order of dismissal and reinstate a temporary restraining order enjoining real party in interest, the Real Estate Commissioner for the State of California (commissioner), from interfering with the developers' sale or other disposition of their real property.


OVERVIEW: The developers were engaged in the subdivision, development, and sale of real property. The commissioner determined that a possible hazardous condition existed in light of evidence that the subdivision's water source facility was inadequate. Accordingly, the commissioner issued a cease and desist order pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10084. The developers sought to bar the enforcement of the cease and desist order. The court issued the alternative writ for the limited purpose of determining whether the legislature acted within its constitutional limitations in enacting § 10084, and if so, whether the means adopted by the commissioner were reasonably designed to achieve the purpose of § 10084. The court denied the developers' petition for a writ of mandamus and held that § 10084 did not deny due process and represented a valid exercise of the police power of the state. Moreover, the court held that the cease and desist order had a real and substantial relation to the objectives sought to be achieved by § 10084.


OUTCOME: The court denied the developers' petition for a writ of mandamus.

Aeration Processes, Inc. v. Jacobsen


Civ. No. 18746


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


184 Cal. App. 2d 836; 8 Cal. Rptr. 85; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1942



September 26, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Albert A. Axelrod, Judge pro tem. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action to enjoin the State from enforcing provisions of Agricultural Code relating to milk products.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed. Judgment for plaintiffs modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the California Director of Agriculture and his subsidiary Bureau of Dairy Service, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which enjoined the State from the enforcement of various provisions of the Agricultural Code against a product manufactured by respondent manufacturers and sold under their trade name, being a dessert topping suitable for use on pies, cakes, and sundaes.


OVERVIEW: The manufacturers, the owners of the trademark and its licensee for manufacture and sale in the San Francisco, California area, filed an action against the State that sought to enjoin the enforcement of various provisions of the California Agricultural Code against them. The trial court found that the topping did not imitate a milk product, found that the statutes were unconstitutional, and enjoined their application. On appeal, the State argued that the use by the manufacturers of nonfat milk solids in its ingredients required the application of Cal. Agric. Code §§ 638, 654, 655, and prevented the manufacture of the topping because it imitated a milk product or adulterated a milk product through the addition of non-milk fats and oils. The court affirmed the essential findings as to non-imitation and inapplicability of § 638 because they were supported by evidence. The court rejected, as surplusage, those portions of the judgment that declared the unconstitutionality of §§ 638, 654, 655 either conditionally by reason of their application to the particular product or by reason of discriminatory enforcement by the State.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court by striking therefrom the paragraphs that declared that the particular code sections were unconstitutional and held that the State officers involved were knowingly and intentionally guilty of unequal and discriminatory enforcement against the manufacturers in favor of segments of the dairy industry. The court affirmed the modified judgment.

People v. O'Hara


Crim. No. 6843


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


184 Cal. App. 2d 798; 8 Cal. Rptr. 114; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1937



September 23, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied November 16, 1960.

Prosecution for grand theft.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of grand theft, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(1), and from an order denying his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and a co-defendant were convicted of grand theft by a jury. They appealed from the judgment and the order denying their motions for a new trial. The co-defendant died, and his appeal was dismissed. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction against defendant. The court found that the information, which charged defendant of unlawfully taking the personal property of another, was sufficient under Cal. Penal Code § 952 to notify defendant of the alleged offense. The evidence was sufficient to support the verdict because the jury could have reasonably inferred from the evidence that defendant was a participant in a certain transaction. No substantial right of defendant was prejudiced by the information being amended to change the date of the alleged offense. The trial court did not err in permitting the prosecution to play a recording of defendant's conversation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

1569 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Adler v. City Council of Culver City


Civ. No. 24514


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


184 Cal. App. 2d 763; 7 Cal. Rptr. 805; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1932



September 22, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied November 16, 1960.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Newcomb Condee, Judge.

Action seeking a declaratory judgment invalidating a city ordinance rezoning certain property and for an injunction against enforcement of the ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of respondent city council in the taxpayers' suit challenging the validity of a zoning ordinance and alleging that the city planning commission (commission) held an illegal secret meeting in violation of the Brown Act, Cal. Gov. Code §§ 54950-54958.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to public notices, public meetings were held by the commission and by the city council on a property owner's application for a zoning change. The commission recommended granting the application with certain conditions. The city council concluded that the commission's conditions were excessive, and adopted an ordinance approving the zoning change with a different set of conditions. The trial court found in favor of the city council and held that the Brown Act, Cal. Gov. Code §§ 54950-54958, did not apply because the city in question was a charter city. On appeal, the court found that the Brown Act did apply to the city but that a dinner meeting hosted by the property owner four days before his zoning application was not an illegal secret meeting because it did not involve a specific discussion of the property owner's plan. Given the formal hearing process that occurred, there was little chance that the dinner meeting influenced the commission and it most likely did not influence the city council at all. An amendment to the city's basic zoning ordinance was valid, though made without prior public notice, because it was legislative, and not administrative, in character.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the city council was affirmed.

 

1570 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Frangadakis


Civ. No. 18828


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


184 Cal. App. 2d 540; 7 Cal. Rptr. 776; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1904



September 13, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. William T. Sweigert, Judge.

Action to abate a public nuisance.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff district attorney filed a complaint for the abatement of a public nuisance pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25604. The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) held that defendants violated § 25604 by maintaining premises for the consumption of alcohol without a license from the department of alcoholic beverage control, and issued an injunction. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The department of alcoholic beverage control's agents and police officers visited defendants' restaurant. They observed that the waitresses served a mix of soda, Seven-up, or water with ice for a charge, and that people poured liquids from bottles that appeared to contain alcoholic beverages. Additionally, the waitresses sold alcoholic beverages to the officers upon request. On appeal, defendants initially challenged the denial of their right to trial by jury. The court found no right to a jury trial in a suit to abate a public nuisance because none existed at common law. Next, the court found no merit in defendants' assertion that evidence of 127 bottles containing alcoholic beverages should have been suppressed because the officers did not have a search warrant. The court found that the evidence was admissible because the search was made after a crime was committed in the officers' presence. The court rejected defendants' contention that the evidence was insufficient to support the finding of a public nuisance. Finally, the court upheld the constitutionality of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25604.


OUTCOME: The court modified the injunction that restrained defendants from maintaining their restaurant's premises for the consumption of alcohol without a license from the department of alcoholic beverage control. As modified, the court affirmed the issuance of the injunction and the judgment.

Yen Eng v. Board of Bldg. & Safety Comm'rs


Civ. No. 24289


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


184 Cal. App. 2d 514; 7 Cal. Rptr. 564; 1960 Cal. App. LEXIS 1899



September 12, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 10, 1960, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied November 9, 1960.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Frank S. Balthis, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a municipal board to set aside an order directing the vacating and demolition of an old building.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent, the Board of Building and Safety Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles, to set aside its order that directed the vacating and demolition of the property owners' building.


OVERVIEW: The property owners had used their wooden seven-story building for residential purposes. The trial court reviewed the board's decision to vacate and demolish and determined that the building was in such a state of deterioration, instability, and so unfit for use, that it was no longer reasonably repairable and amounted to a public nuisance, which could only be reasonably abated by demolition. On appeal, the court held that the board's order was supported by ample persuasive evidence to justify its order to demolish as the only way to abate the nuisance. The property owners offered testimony as to the estimated cost of repairs to put the building in a reasonably safe condition. The court noted a great disparity in estimates to repair the building, and determined that the public nuisance could not be reasonably abated by means other than demolition because even the largest sum would not bring it up to the safety requirements. The court held that the municipal code standards were constitutional because they were reasonable and substantially related to their object of protecting public health, safety and general welfare, and the board's order was a proper exercise of police power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying the building owners' petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to compel the board to set aside its order of demolition.
